<div>Image of Ganymede's anti-Jovian hemisphere taken by the <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> orbiter (contrast is enhanced). Lighter surfaces, such as in recent impacts, grooved terrain and the whitish north polar cap at upper right, are enriched in water ice.</div>

<p><b>Ganymede</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/ or /ə/ 'e' in 'roses'">ᵻ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/iː/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iː</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-respell_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-respell-18">[12]</a></sup> (<b>Jupiter III</b>) is the largest <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">moon of Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">in the Solar System</a>, and the only <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moon</a> known to have a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>. It is the seventh satellite outward from <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_Society_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary_Society-19">[13]</a></sup> and third of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a>, the first group of objects discovered orbiting another planet. Ganymede <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> Jupiter in roughly seven days and is in a 1:2:4 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonance</a> with the moons <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, respectively. Ganymede has a diameter of 5,268 km (3,273 mi), 8 % larger than the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, but its mass is only 45 % that of Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa.gany_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gany-20">[14]</a></sup> Ganymede is 2 % larger than Saturn's <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> (second-largest moon of the Solar System). At 2.02 times the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, it is the most massive planetary satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplanets.org-Ganymede_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-nineplanets.org-Ganymede-21">[15]</a></sup> It is the ninth-largest object in the Solar System, and the largest without a substantial atmosphere.</p>
<p>Ganymede is composed of approximately equal amounts of <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate rock</a> and water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150315_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150315-22">[16]</a></sup> It is a fully <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiated</a> body with an iron-rich, liquid core, and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_ocean" title="Internal ocean">internal ocean</a> that may contain more water than all of Earth's oceans combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ocean_Hubble_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ocean_Hubble-23">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RT_Ganymede_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-RT_Ganymede-24">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clubsandwich_2014_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-clubsandwich_2014-25">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vance_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vance-26">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140501c_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140501c-27">[21]</a></sup> Its surface is composed of two main types of terrain. Dark regions, saturated with <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a> and dated to four billion years ago, cover about a third of the satellite. Lighter regions, crosscut by extensive grooves and ridges and only slightly less ancient, cover the remainder. The cause of the light terrain's disrupted geology is not fully known, but was likely the result of <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic</a> activity due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ganymede's magnetic field is probably created by <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> within its liquid iron core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> The meager magnetic field is buried within Jupiter's much larger <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> and would show only as a local perturbation of the <a href="/wiki/Field_line" title="Field line">field lines</a>. The satellite has a thin <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> that includes O, O<sub>2</sub>, and possibly O<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall1998_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hall1998-17">[11]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen" title="Atomic hydrogen">Atomic hydrogen</a> is a minor atmospheric constituent. Whether the satellite has an <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> associated with its atmosphere is unresolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eviatar2001_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eviatar2001-29">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ganymede's discovery is credited to <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, who was the first to observe it on January 7, 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SidereusNuncius_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-SidereusNuncius-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-2">[2]</a></sup> The satellite's name was soon suggested by astronomer <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a>, for the mythological <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(mythology)" title="Ganymede (mythology)">Ganymede</a>, cupbearer of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek gods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>'s lover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naming_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naming-30">[24]</a></sup> Beginning with <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i>, spacecraft have been able to examine Ganymede closely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pioneer_11_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pioneer_11-31">[25]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a></i> probes refined measurements of its size, whereas the <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> craft discovered its underground ocean and magnetic field. The next planned mission to the Jovian system is the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer</a> (JUICE), due to launch in 2022. After flybys of all three icy Galilean moons, the probe is planned to enter orbit around Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selection_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-selection-32">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Chinese astronomical records report that in 365 BC, <a href="/wiki/Gan_De" title="Gan De">Gan De</a> detected what appears to have been a moon of Jupiter, probably Ganymede, with the naked eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ads793c_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-ads793c-33">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ads.793b_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ads.793b-34">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 7, 1610, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> observed what he thought were three stars near Jupiter, including what turned out to be Ganymede, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, and one body that turned out to be the combined light from <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>; the next night he noticed that they had moved. On January 13, he saw all four at once for the first time, but had seen each of the moons before this date at least once. By January 15, Galileo came to the conclusion that the stars were actually bodies orbiting Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SidereusNuncius_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-SidereusNuncius-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-3">[3]</a></sup> He claimed the right to name the moons; he considered "Cosmian Stars" and settled on "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_Satellites#Dedication_to_the_Medicis" title="Galilean Satellites">Medicean Stars</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naming_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naming-30">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ganymede%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/250px-Ganymede%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ganymede%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/375px-Ganymede%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ganymede%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/500px-Ganymede%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Size comparison of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, and Ganymede.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The French astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nicolas-Claude_Fabri_de_Peiresc" title="Nicolas-Claude Fabri de Peiresc">Nicolas-Claude Fabri de Peiresc</a> suggested individual names from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medici" title="Medici">Medici</a> family for the moons, but his proposal was not taken up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naming_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Naming-30">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a>, who had originally claimed to have found the Galilean satellites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-College_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-College-35">[29]</a></sup> tried to name the moons the "Saturn of Jupiter", the "Jupiter of Jupiter" (this was Ganymede), the "Venus of Jupiter", and the "Mercury of Jupiter", another nomenclature that never caught on. From a suggestion by <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>, Marius once again tried to name the moons:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naming_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Naming-30">[24]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>“[…] Then there was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganymede_(Greek_mythology)" title="Ganymede (Greek mythology)">Ganymede</a>, the handsome son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Tros" title="King Tros">King Tros</a>, whom Jupiter, having taken the form of an eagle, transported to heaven on his back, as poets fabulously tell […] the Third, on account of its majesty of light, Ganymede […]”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discovery_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discovery-36">[30]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>This name and those of the other Galilean satellites fell into disfavor for a considerable time, and were not in common use until the mid-20th century. In much of the earlier astronomical literature, Ganymede is referred to instead by its Roman numeral designation, <span class="nowrap">Jupiter III</span> (a system introduced by Galileo), in other words "the third satellite of Jupiter". Following the discovery of moons of Saturn, a naming system based on that of Kepler and Marius was used for Jupiter's moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naming_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Naming-30">[24]</a></sup> Ganymede is the only Galilean moon of Jupiter named after a male figure—like Io, Europa, and Callisto, he was a lover of Zeus.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="245" data-file-width="365" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif/300px-Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/83/Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif 1.5x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galilean_moon_Laplace_resonance_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplace_resonance" title="Laplace resonance">Laplace resonances</a> of Ganymede, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ganymede <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> Jupiter at a distance of 1,070,400 km, third among the Galilean satellites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planetary_Society_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Planetary_Society-19">[13]</a></sup> and completes a revolution every seven days and three hours. Like most known moons, Ganymede is <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">tidally locked</a>, with one side always facing toward the planet, hence its day is seven days and three hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Grand_Tour_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Grand_Tour-37">[31]</a></sup> Its orbit is very slightly eccentric and inclined to the Jovian <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclination</a> changing <a href="/wiki/Almost_periodic_function" title="Almost periodic function">quasi-periodically</a> due to solar and planetary gravitational <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbations</a> on a timescale of centuries. The ranges of change are 0.0009–0.0022 and 0.05–0.32°, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musotto2002_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Musotto2002-38">[32]</a></sup> These orbital variations cause the <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> (the angle between rotational and orbital axes) to vary between 0 and 0.33°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bills2005_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bills2005-12">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ganymede participates in <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonances</a> with Europa and Io: for every orbit of Ganymede, Europa orbits twice and Io orbits four times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musotto2002_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Musotto2002-38">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPACE.com_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPACE.com-39">[33]</a></sup> The superior <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_conjunction" title="Astronomical conjunction">conjunction</a> between Io and Europa always occurs when Io is at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">periapsis</a> and Europa at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoapsis" title="Apoapsis">apoapsis</a>. The superior conjunction between Europa and Ganymede occurs when Europa is at periapsis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musotto2002_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Musotto2002-38">[32]</a></sup> The longitudes of the Io–Europa and Europa–Ganymede conjunctions change with the same rate, making the triple conjunctions impossible. Such a complicated resonance is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laplace_resonance" title="Laplace resonance">Laplace resonance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1102" data-file-width="1102" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg/200px-Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg/300px-Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg/400px-Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jupiter%27s_Great_Red_Spot_and_Ganymede%27s_shadow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jupiter's Great Red Spot and Ganymede's shadow<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cwgjupgany_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-cwgjupgany-41">[35]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The current Laplace resonance is unable to pump the orbital eccentricity of Ganymede to a higher value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup> The value of about 0.0013 is probably a remnant from a previous epoch, when such pumping was possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPACE.com_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPACE.com-39">[33]</a></sup> The Ganymedian orbital eccentricity is somewhat puzzling; if it is not pumped now it should have decayed long ago due to the tidal <a href="/wiki/Dissipation" title="Dissipation">dissipation</a> in the interior of Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup> This means that the last episode of the eccentricity excitation happened only several hundred million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-3"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup> Because Ganymede's orbital eccentricity is relatively low—on average 0.0015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPACE.com_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-SPACE.com-39">[33]</a></sup>—<a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a> is negligible now.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-4"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup> However, in the past Ganymede may have passed through one or more Laplace-like resonances<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laplaceres_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-laplaceres-42">[g]</a></sup> that were able to pump the orbital eccentricity to a value as high as 0.01–0.02.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-5"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup> This probably caused a significant tidal heating of the interior of Ganymede; the formation of the grooved terrain may be a result of one or more heating episodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-6"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are two hypotheses for the origin of the Laplace resonance among Io, Europa, and Ganymede: that it is primordial and has existed from the beginning of the Solar System;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peale2002_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peale2002-43">[36]</a></sup> or that it developed after the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation of the Solar System</a>. A possible sequence of events for the latter scenario is as follows: Io raised tides on Jupiter, causing Io's orbit to expand (due to conservation of momentum) until it encountered the 2:1 resonance with Europa; after that the expansion continued, but some of the angular <a href="/wiki/Moment_(physics)" title="Moment (physics)">moment</a> was transferred to Europa as the resonance caused its orbit to expand as well; the process continued until Europa encountered the 2:1 resonance with Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-7"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup> Eventually the drift rates of conjunctions between all three moons were synchronized and locked in the Laplace resonance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997a_40-8"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997a-40">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg/200px-Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg/300px-Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg/400px-Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon_Ganymede_by_NOAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of Ganymede centered over 45° W. longitude; dark areas are Perrine (upper) and Nicholson (lower) regiones; prominent craters are Tros (upper right) and Cisti (lower left).</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ganymede is the largest moon in the solar system with a diameter of 5,268 km. Its diameter is 1.51 times the Moon's, 1.08 times Mercury's, and 0.41 times that of Earth.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The average <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of Ganymede, 1.936 <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">g</a>/cm<sup>3</sup>, suggests a composition of about equal parts rocky material and mostly water-ices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mass_fraction_(chemistry)" title="Mass fraction (chemistry)">mass fraction</a> of ices is between 46–50 %, which is slightly lower than that in Callisto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-44">[37]</a></sup> Some additional volatile ices such as <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> may also be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-44">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn2003_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn2003-45">[38]</a></sup> The exact composition of Ganymede's <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a> is not known, but is probably close to the composition of <a href="/wiki/L_chondrite" title="L chondrite">L</a>/<a href="/wiki/LL_chondrite" title="LL chondrite">LL type</a> <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_chondrite" title="Ordinary chondrite">ordinary chondrites</a>, which are characterized by less total <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, less metallic iron and more <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">iron oxide</a> than <a href="/wiki/H_chondrite" title="H chondrite">H chondrites</a>. The weight ratio of iron to <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> ranges between 1.05 and 1.27 in Ganymede, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">solar ratio</a> is around 1.8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-44">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1860" data-file-width="2000" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg/200px-PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg/300px-PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg/400px-PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA00081_Ganymede_Voyager_2_mosaic.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> view of Ganymede's anti-Jovian hemisphere; <a href="/wiki/Uruk_Sulcus" title="Uruk Sulcus">Uruk Sulcus</a> separates dark areas <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Regio" title="Galileo Regio">Galileo Regio</a> (right) and Marius Regio (center left). Bright rays of recent crater <a href="/wiki/List_of_craters_on_Ganymede" title="List of craters on Ganymede">Osiris</a> (bottom) are ejected ice.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">Surface features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Surface features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ganymede's surface has an <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> of about 43 %.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvin1995_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calvin1995-46">[39]</a></sup> Water ice seems to be ubiquitous on its surface, with a mass fraction of 50–90 %,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> significantly more than in Ganymede as a whole. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">Near-infrared</a> <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> has revealed the presence of strong water ice <a href="/wiki/Absorption_band" title="Absorption band">absorption bands</a> at wavelengths of 1.04, 1.25, 1.5, 2.0 and 3.0 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">μm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvin1995_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calvin1995-46">[39]</a></sup> The grooved terrain is brighter and has a more icy composition than the dark terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RESA_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-RESA-47">[40]</a></sup> The analysis of high-resolution, near-infrared and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a><a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectra</a> obtained by the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)"><i>Galileo</i></a> spacecraft and from Earth observations has revealed various non-water materials: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> and, possibly, <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfate" title="Hydrogen sulfate">hydrogen sulfate</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord1998_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCord1998-48">[41]</a></sup> <i>Galileo</i> results have also shown <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_sulfate" title="Magnesium sulfate">magnesium sulfate</a> (MgSO<sub>4</sub>) and, possibly, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_sulfate" title="Sodium sulfate">sodium sulfate</a> (Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) on Ganymede's surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Grand_Tour_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Grand_Tour-37">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2001_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2001-49">[42]</a></sup> These salts may originate from the subsurface ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2001_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2001-49">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ganymedian surface albedo is very asymmetric; the leading hemisphere<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hemispherecomment_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-hemispherecomment-50">[h]</a></sup> is brighter than the trailing one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvin1995_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Calvin1995-46">[39]</a></sup> This is similar to Europa, but the reverse for Callisto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calvin1995_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-Calvin1995-46">[39]</a></sup> The trailing hemisphere of Ganymede appears to be enriched in sulfur dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Domingue1996_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Domingue1996-51">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Domingue1998_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Domingue1998-52">[44]</a></sup> The distribution of carbon dioxide does not demonstrate any hemispheric asymmetry, although it is not observed near the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord1998_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-McCord1998-48">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbitts2003_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbitts2003-53">[45]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">Impact craters</a> on Ganymede (except one) do not show any enrichment in carbon dioxide, which also distinguishes it from Callisto. Ganymede's carbon dioxide gas was probably depleted in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbitts2003_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hibbitts2003-53">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Ganymede" title="List of geological features on Ganymede">List of geological features on Ganymede</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede-moon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="687" data-file-width="687" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ganymede-moon.jpg/200px-Ganymede-moon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ganymede-moon.jpg/300px-Ganymede-moon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ganymede-moon.jpg/400px-Ganymede-moon.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

Enhanced-color <i>Galileo</i> spacecraft image of Ganymede's trailing hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spaceflight_Now_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spaceflight_Now-54">[46]</a></sup> The crater Tashmetum's prominent rays are at lower right, and the large ejecta field of Hershef at upper right. Part of dark Nicholson Regio is at lower left, bounded on its upper right by Harpagia Sulcus.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="753" data-file-width="813" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg/200px-Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg/300px-Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg/400px-Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Craters_on_Ganymede.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The craters <a href="/wiki/Gula_(crater)" title="Gula (crater)">Gula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Achelous_(crater)" title="Achelous (crater)">Achelous</a> (bottom), in the grooved terrain of Ganymede, with <a href="/wiki/Ejecta_blanket" title="Ejecta blanket">ejecta</a> "<a href="/wiki/Pedestal_crater" title="Pedestal crater">pedestals</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Rampart_crater" title="Rampart crater">ramparts</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ganymede's surface is a mix of two types of terrain: very old, highly <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">cratered</a>, dark regions and somewhat younger (but still ancient), lighter regions marked with an extensive array of grooves and ridges. The dark terrain, which comprises about one-third of the surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Patterson2007_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Patterson2007-55">[47]</a></sup> contains clays and organic materials that could indicate the composition of the impactors from which Jovian satellites accreted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pappalardo2001_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pappalardo2001-56">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>The heating mechanism required for the formation of the grooved terrain on Ganymede is an unsolved problem in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_sciences" title="Planetary sciences">planetary sciences</a>. The modern view is that the grooved terrain is mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">tectonic</a> in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">Cryovolcanism</a> is thought to have played only a minor role, if any.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> The forces that caused the strong stresses in the Ganymedian ice <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> necessary to initiate the tectonic activity may be connected to the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">tidal heating</a> events in the past, possibly caused when the satellite passed through unstable <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">orbital resonances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1997b_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1997b-57">[49]</a></sup> The tidal flexing of the ice may have heated the interior and strained the lithosphere, leading to the development of cracks and <a href="/wiki/Horst_and_graben" title="Horst and graben">horst and graben</a> faulting, which erased the old, dark terrain on 70 % of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bland2007_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bland2007-58">[50]</a></sup> The formation of the grooved terrain may also be connected with the early core formation and subsequent tidal heating of Ganymede's interior, which may have caused a slight expansion of Ganymede by 1–6 % due to <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transitions</a> in ice and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-14"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> During subsequent evolution deep, hot water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plume_(hydrodynamics)" title="Plume (hydrodynamics)">plumes</a> may have risen from the core to the surface, leading to the tectonic deformation of the lithosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barr-59">[51]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic_heat" title="Radiogenic heat">Radiogenic heating</a> within the satellite is the most relevant current heat source, contributing, for instance, to ocean depth. Research models have found that if the orbital eccentricity were an order of magnitude greater than currently (as it may have been in the past), tidal heating would be a more substantial heat source than radiogenic heating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gra.6_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-gra.6-60">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cratering is seen on both types of terrain, but is especially extensive on the dark terrain: it appears to be saturated with impact craters and has evolved largely through impact events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-15"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> The brighter, grooved terrain contains many fewer impact features, which have been only of a minor importance to its tectonic evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-16"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> The density of cratering indicates an age of 4 billion years for the dark terrain, similar to the highlands of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, and a somewhat younger age for the grooved terrain (but how much younger is uncertain).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle1998_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zahnle1998-61">[53]</a></sup> Ganymede may have experienced a period of heavy cratering 3.5 to 4 billion years ago similar to that of the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle1998_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zahnle1998-61">[53]</a></sup> If true, the vast majority of impacts happened in that epoch, whereas the cratering rate has been much smaller since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplanets.org-Ganymede_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-nineplanets.org-Ganymede-21">[15]</a></sup> Craters both overlay and are crosscut by the groove systems, indicating that some of the grooves are quite ancient. Relatively young craters with rays of ejecta are also visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplanets.org-Ganymede_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-nineplanets.org-Ganymede-21">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganymede_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ganymede-62">[54]</a></sup> Ganymedian craters are flatter than those on the Moon and Mercury. This is probably due to the relatively weak nature of Ganymede's icy crust, which can (or could) flow and thereby soften the relief. Ancient craters whose relief has disappeared leave only a "ghost" of a crater known as a <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest_(planetary_astronomy)" title="Palimpsest (planetary astronomy)">palimpsest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nineplanets.org-Ganymede_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-nineplanets.org-Ganymede-21">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>One significant feature on Ganymede is a dark plain named <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Regio" title="Galileo Regio">Galileo Regio</a>, which contains a series of concentric grooves, or furrows, likely created during a period of geologic activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Casacchia_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Casacchia-63">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ganymede also has polar caps, likely composed of water frost. The frost extends to 40° latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Grand_Tour_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Grand_Tour-37">[31]</a></sup> These polar caps were first seen by the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i></a> spacecraft. Theories on the formation of the caps include the migration of water to higher latitudes and bombardment of the ice by plasma. Data from <i>Galileo</i> suggests the latter is correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar_caps_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polar_caps-64">[56]</a></sup> The presence of a magnetic field on Ganymede results in more intense charged particle bombardment of its surface in the unprotected polar regions; sputtering then leads to redistribution of water molecules, with frost migrating to locally colder areas within the polar terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polar_caps_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polar_caps-64">[56]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:808px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png" title="Geologic map of Ganymede (February 2014). The oldest, low-albedo, cratered units are reddish brown; younger, higher-albedo units are blue if grooved and blue-green if smooth (purple is a mix of grooved and smooth)."><img alt="" data-file-height="4099" data-file-width="5801" height="565" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png/800px-Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png/1200px-Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png/1600px-Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png 2x" width="800"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png" title="File:Ganymede-JupiterMoon-GeologicMap-SIM3237-20140211.png"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Geologic_map" title="Geologic map">Geologic map</a> of Ganymede (February 2014). The oldest, low-albedo, cratered units are reddish brown; younger, higher-albedo units are blue if grooved and blue-green if smooth (purple is a mix of grooved and smooth).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A crater named <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Anat_(crater)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Anat (crater) (page does not exist)">Anat</a> provides the reference point for measuring longitude on Ganymede. By definition, Anat is at 128° longitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau.table2_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-iau.table2-65">[57]</a></sup> The 0° longitude directly faces Jupiter, and unless stated otherwise longitude increases toward the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-targetcoordsys_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-targetcoordsys-66">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Internal structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ganymede appears to be fully <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiated</a>, with an internal structure consisting of an <a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfide" title="Iron(II) sulfide">iron-sulfide</a>–<a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">core</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> and outer layers of water ice and liquid water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-17"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohl2002_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sohl2002-67">[59]</a></sup> The precise thicknesses of the different layers in the interior of Ganymede depend on the assumed composition of silicates (fraction of <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a>) and amount of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> in the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-44">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohl2002_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sohl2002-67">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005b_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005b-68">[60]</a></sup> Ganymede has the lowest <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia_factor" title="Moment of inertia factor">moment of inertia factor</a>, 0.31,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-18"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> among the solid Solar System bodies. This is a consequence of its substantial water content and fully differentiated interior.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsurface_oceans">Subsurface oceans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Subsurface oceans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2800" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Ganymede_diagram.svg/400px-Ganymede_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Ganymede_diagram.svg/600px-Ganymede_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Ganymede_diagram.svg/800px-Ganymede_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's cut-away representation of the internal structure of Ganymede. Layers drawn to scale.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1970s, NASA scientists first suspected that Ganymede has a thick ocean between two layers of ice, one on the surface and one beneath a liquid ocean and atop the rocky mantle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-19"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clubsandwich_2014_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-clubsandwich_2014-25">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohl2002_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sohl2002-67">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-69">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amount_of_water_in_ocean_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-amount_of_water_in_ocean-70">[62]</a></sup> In the 1990s, NASA's <i>Galileo</i> mission flew by Ganymede, confirming the moon's sub-surface ocean. An analysis published in 2014, taking into account the realistic thermodynamics for water and effects of salt, suggests that Ganymede might have a stack of several ocean layers separated by different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phases_of_ice" title="Phases of ice">phases of ice</a>, with the lowest liquid layer adjacent to the rocky <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clubsandwich_2014_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-clubsandwich_2014-25">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vance_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vance-26">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140501c_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140501c-27">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_2015_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble_2015-71">[63]</a></sup> Water–rock contact may be an important factor in the <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">origin of life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clubsandwich_2014_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-clubsandwich_2014-25">[19]</a></sup> The analysis also notes that the extreme depths involved (~800 km to the rocky "seafloor") mean that temperatures at the bottom of a convective (adiabatic) ocean can be up to 40 K higher than those at the ice–water interface. In March 2015, scientists reported that measurements with the Hubble Space Telescope of how the aurorae moved over Ganymede's surface suggest it has a subsurface ocean. A large salt-water ocean affects Ganymede's magnetic field, and consequently, its aurora.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ocean_Hubble_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ocean_Hubble-23">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_2015_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble_2015-71">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily1503_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily1503-72">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sswater1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-sswater1-73">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is some speculation on the potential <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitability</a> of Ganymede's ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amount_of_water_in_ocean_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-amount_of_water_in_ocean-70">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subsurface_ocean_found_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-subsurface_ocean_found-74">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Core">Core</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Core">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The existence of a liquid, <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy" title="Iron–nickel alloy">iron–nickel</a>-rich core provides a natural explanation for the intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetic field</a> of Ganymede detected by <i>Galileo</i> spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> in the liquid iron, which has high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, is the most reasonable model of magnetic field generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> The density of the core is 5.5–6 g/cm<sup>3</sup> and the silicate mantle is 3.4–3.6 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-44">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohl2002_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sohl2002-67">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005b_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005b-68">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> The radius of this core may be up to 500 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> The temperature in the core of Ganymede is probably 1500–1700 K and pressure up to 10 GPa (99,000 atm) (<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohl2002_67-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sohl2002-67">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere_and_ionosphere">Atmosphere and ionosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Atmosphere and ionosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1972, a team of Indian, British and American astronomers working in Java (Indonesia) and Kavalur (India) claimed that they had detected a thin atmosphere during an <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a>, when it and Jupiter passed in front of a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson1973_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson1973-76">[68]</a></sup> They estimated that the surface pressure was around 0.1 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson1973_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carlson1973-76">[68]</a></sup> However, in 1979, <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> observed an occultation of the star <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Centauri" title="Kappa Centauri">κ Centauri</a> during its flyby of Jupiter, with differing results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadfoot1981_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broadfoot1981-77">[69]</a></sup> The occultation measurements were conducted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far-ultraviolet" title="Far-ultraviolet">far-ultraviolet</a> spectrum at <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> shorter than 200 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a>, which were much more sensitive to the presence of gases than the 1972 measurements made in the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>. No atmosphere was revealed by the <i>Voyager</i> data. The upper limit on the surface particle <a href="/wiki/Number_density" title="Number density">number density</a> was found to be <span class="nowrap">1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup> cm<sup>−3</sup></span>, which corresponds to a surface pressure of less than 2.5 µPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadfoot1981_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Broadfoot1981-77">[69]</a></sup> The latter value is almost five orders of magnitude less than the 1972 estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadfoot1981_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Broadfoot1981-77">[69]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="432" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg/200px-Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg/300px-Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg/400px-Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_temparatureof_ganymede.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
False-color temperature map of Ganymede</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the <i>Voyager</i> data, evidence for a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> atmosphere (<a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>) on Ganymede, very similar to the one found on <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, was found by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> (HST) in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall1998_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hall1998-17">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPLAtmosphere_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPLAtmosphere-78">[70]</a></sup> HST actually observed <a href="/wiki/Airglow" title="Airglow">airglow</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_oxygen" title="Atomic oxygen">atomic oxygen</a> in the far-ultraviolet at the wavelengths 130.4 nm and 135.6 nm. Such an airglow is excited when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" title="Molecular oxygen">molecular oxygen</a> is <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociated</a> by electron impacts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall1998_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hall1998-17">[11]</a></sup> which is evidence of a significant neutral atmosphere composed predominantly of O<sub>2</sub> molecules. The surface number density probably lies in the <span class="nowrap">(1.2–7)<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">8</span></sup> cm<sup>−3</sup></span> range, corresponding to the surface pressure of <span class="nowrap">0.2–1.2 µPa</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall1998_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hall1998-17">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surfacedensitynumber_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-surfacedensitynumber-79">[i]</a></sup> These values are in agreement with the <i>Voyager</i>'s upper limit set in 1981. The oxygen is not evidence of life; it is thought to be produced when water ice on Ganymede's surface is split into <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and oxygen by radiation, with the hydrogen then being more rapidly lost due to its low atomic mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPLAtmosphere_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-JPLAtmosphere-78">[70]</a></sup> The airglow observed over Ganymede is not spatially homogeneous like that over Europa. HST observed two bright spots located in the northern and southern hemispheres, near ± 50° latitude, which is exactly the boundary between the open and closed field lines of the Ganymedian magnetosphere (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman2000_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman2000-80">[71]</a></sup> The bright spots are probably polar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">auroras</a>, caused by plasma precipitation along the open field lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson1997_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1997-81">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>The existence of a neutral atmosphere implies that an <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> should exist, because oxygen molecules are ionized by the impacts of the energetic <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> coming from the magnetosphere<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paranicas1999_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paranicas1999-82">[73]</a></sup> and by solar <a href="/wiki/Extreme_ultraviolet" title="Extreme ultraviolet">EUV</a> radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eviatar2001_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eviatar2001-29">[23]</a></sup> However, the nature of the Ganymedian ionosphere is as controversial as the nature of the atmosphere. Some <i>Galileo</i> measurements found an elevated electron density near Ganymede, suggesting an ionosphere, whereas others failed to detect anything.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eviatar2001_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eviatar2001-29">[23]</a></sup> The electron density near the surface is estimated by different sources to lie in the range 400–2,500 cm<sup>−3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eviatar2001_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eviatar2001-29">[23]</a></sup> As of 2008, the parameters of the ionosphere of Ganymede are not well constrained.</p>
<p>Additional evidence of the oxygen atmosphere comes from spectral detection of gases trapped in the ice at the surface of Ganymede. The detection of <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> (O<sub>3</sub>) bands was announced in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noll1996_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noll1996-83">[74]</a></sup> In 1997 spectroscopic analysis revealed the <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatomic" title="Diatomic">diatomic</a>) absorption features of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" title="Molecular oxygen">molecular oxygen</a>. Such an absorption can arise only if the oxygen is in a dense phase. The best candidate is molecular oxygen trapped in ice. The depth of the dimer absorption bands depends on <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, rather than on surface <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>—they tend to decrease with increasing latitude on Ganymede, whereas O<sub>3</sub> shows an opposite trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxygen97_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxygen97-84">[75]</a></sup> Laboratory work has found that O<sub>2</sub> would not cluster or bubble but dissolve in ice at Ganymede's relatively warm surface temperature of 100 K (−173.15 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sci.5320_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-sci.5320-85">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>A search for <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> in the atmosphere, just after such a finding on Europa, turned up nothing in 1997. Sodium is at least 13 times less abundant around Ganymede than around Europa, possibly because of a relative deficiency at the surface or because the magnetosphere fends off energetic particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ic.126.1_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-ic.126.1-86">[77]</a></sup> Another minor constituent of the Ganymedian atmosphere is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_hydrogen" title="Atomic hydrogen">atomic hydrogen</a>. Hydrogen atoms were observed as far as 3,000 km from Ganymede's surface. Their density on the surface is about <span class="nowrap">1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">4</span></sup> cm<sup>−3</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barth1997_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barth1997-87">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Magnetosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="631" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg/220px-Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg/330px-Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg/440px-Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede_magnetic_field.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Magnetic field of the Jovian satellite Ganymede, which is embedded into the magnetosphere of Jupiter. Closed field lines are marked with green color.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <i>Galileo</i> craft made six close flybys of Ganymede from 1995–2000 (G1, G2, G7, G8, G28 and G29)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> and discovered that Ganymede has a permanent (intrinsic) <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a> independent of the Jovian magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1997_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1997-88">[79]</a></sup> The value of the moment is about <span class="nowrap">1.3 × 10<sup>13</sup> T·m<sup>3</sup></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> which is three times larger than the <a href="/wiki/Mercury%27s_magnetic_field" title="Mercury's magnetic field">magnetic moment of Mercury</a>. The magnetic dipole is tilted with respect to the rotational axis of Ganymede by 176°, which means that it is directed against the Jovian magnetic moment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> Its north pole lies below the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_plane_(astronomy)" title="Orbital plane (astronomy)">orbital plane</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole magnetic field</a> created by this permanent moment has a strength of 719 ± 2 <a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">nT</a> at Ganymede's equator,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> which should be compared with the Jovian magnetic field at the distance of Ganymede—about 120 nT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1997_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1997-88">[79]</a></sup> The equatorial field of Ganymede is directed against the Jovian field, meaning <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection" title="Magnetic reconnection">reconnection</a> is possible. The intrinsic field strength at the poles is two times that at the equator—1440 nT.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
