</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png/200px-15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png/300px-15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png/400px-15-33i2-JupiterMoon-Ganymede-Aurora-20150312.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Aurorae on Ganymede—auroral belt shifting may indicate a subsurface saline ocean.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The permanent magnetic moment carves a part of space around Ganymede, creating a tiny <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> embedded inside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter%27s_magnetosphere" title="Jupiter's magnetosphere">that of Jupiter</a>; it is the only moon in the Solar System known to possess the feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1997_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1997-88">[79]</a></sup> Its diameter is 4–5 <i>R</i><sub>G</sub> (<i>R</i><sub>G</sub> = 2,631.2 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1998_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1998-89">[80]</a></sup> The Ganymedian magnetosphere has a region of closed <a href="/wiki/Field_line" title="Field line">field lines</a> located below 30° <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">charged particles</a> (<a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>) are trapped, creating a kind of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_belt" title="Radiation belt">radiation belt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1998_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1998-89">[80]</a></sup> The main ion species in the magnetosphere is single ionized <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>—O<sup>+</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eviatar2001_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eviatar2001-29">[23]</a></sup>—which fits well with Ganymede's tenuous oxygen <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. In the polar cap regions, at latitudes higher than 30°, magnetic field lines are open, connecting Ganymede with Jupiter's <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1998_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1998-89">[80]</a></sup> In these areas, the energetic (tens and hundreds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiloelectronvolt" title="Kiloelectronvolt">kiloelectronvolt</a>) electrons and ions have been detected,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paranicas1999_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Paranicas1999-82">[73]</a></sup> which may cause the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">auroras</a> observed around the Ganymedian poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman2000_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman2000-80">[71]</a></sup> In addition, heavy ions continuously precipitate on Ganymede's polar surface, <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">sputtering</a> and darkening the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paranicas1999_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Paranicas1999-82">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The interaction between the Ganymedian magnetosphere and Jovian <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> is in many respects similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> and Earth's magnetosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson1998_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson1998-89">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volwerk1999_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Volwerk1999-90">[81]</a></sup> The plasma co-rotating with Jupiter impinges on the trailing side of the Ganymedian magnetosphere much like the solar wind impinges on the Earth's magnetosphere. The main difference is the speed of plasma flow—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> in the case of Earth and <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">subsonic</a> in the case of Ganymede. Because of the subsonic flow, there is no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a> off the trailing hemisphere of Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volwerk1999_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Volwerk1999-90">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the intrinsic magnetic moment, Ganymede has an induced dipole magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> Its existence is connected with the variation of the Jovian magnetic field near Ganymede. The induced moment is directed radially to or from Jupiter following the direction of the varying part of the planetary magnetic field. The induced magnetic moment is an order of magnitude weaker than the intrinsic one. The <a href="/wiki/Field_strength" title="Field strength">field strength</a> of the induced field at the magnetic equator is about 60 nT—half of that of the ambient Jovian field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> The induced magnetic field of Ganymede is similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, indicating that Ganymede also has a subsurface water ocean with a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Given that Ganymede is completely differentiated and has a metallic core,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-20"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> its intrinsic magnetic field is probably generated in a similar fashion to the Earth's: as a result of conducting material moving in the interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> The magnetic field detected around Ganymede is likely to be caused by compositional convection in the core,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> if the magnetic field is the product of dynamo action, or magnetoconvection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauck2002_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hauck2002-91">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the presence of an iron core, Ganymede's magnetosphere remains enigmatic, particularly given that similar bodies lack the feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-21"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup> Some research has suggested that, given its relatively small size, the core ought to have sufficiently cooled to the point where fluid motions, hence a magnetic field would not be sustained. One explanation is that the same orbital resonances proposed to have disrupted the surface also allowed the magnetic field to persist: with Ganymede's eccentricity pumped and tidal heating of the mantle increased during such resonances, reducing heat flow from the core, leaving it fluid and convective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bland2007_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bland2007-58">[50]</a></sup> Another explanation is a remnant magnetization of silicate rocks in the mantle, which is possible if the satellite had a more significant dynamo-generated field in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_7-22"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-7">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">Origin and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ganymede probably formed by an <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> in Jupiter's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">subnebula</a>, a disk of gas and dust surrounding Jupiter after its formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2002_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2002-92">[83]</a></sup> The accretion of Ganymede probably took about 10,000 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosqueira2003_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosqueira2003-93">[84]</a></sup> much shorter than the 100,000 years estimated for <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>. The Jovian subnebula may have been relatively "gas-starved" when the Galilean satellites formed; this would have allowed for the lengthy accretion times required for Callisto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2002_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canup2002-92">[83]</a></sup> In contrast Ganymede formed closer to Jupiter, where the subnebula was denser, which explains its shorter formation timescale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosqueira2003_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mosqueira2003-93">[84]</a></sup> This relatively fast formation prevented the escape of accretional heat, which may have led to ice melt and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiation</a>: the separation of the rocks and ice. The rocks settled to the center, forming the core. In this respect, Ganymede is different from Callisto, which apparently failed to melt and differentiate early due to loss of the accretional heat during its slower formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-94">[85]</a></sup> This hypothesis explains why the two Jovian moons look so dissimilar, despite their similar mass and composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-69">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-94">[85]</a></sup> Alternative theories explain Ganymede's greater internal heating on the basis of tidal flexing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman2_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Showman2-95">[86]</a></sup> or more intense pummeling by impactors during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-96">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPI1158_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-LPI1158-97">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr3_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barr3-98">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>After formation, Ganymede's core largely retained the heat accumulated during accretion and differentiation, only slowly releasing it to the ice mantle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-94">[85]</a></sup> The mantle, in turn, transported it to the surface by convection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-69">[61]</a></sup> The decay of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactive elements</a> within rocks further heated the core, causing increased differentiation: an inner, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>–<a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfide" title="Iron(II) sulfide">iron-sulfide</a> core and a <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> mantle formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-7"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-94">[85]</a></sup> With this, Ganymede became a fully differentiated body. By comparison, the radioactive heating of undifferentiated Callisto caused convection in its icy interior, which effectively cooled it and prevented large-scale melting of ice and rapid differentiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel2004_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel2004-99">[90]</a></sup> The convective motions in Callisto have caused only a partial separation of rock and ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel2004_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel2004-99">[90]</a></sup> Today, Ganymede continues to cool slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-8"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> The heat being released from its core and silicate mantle enables the subsurface ocean to exist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn2003_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spohn2003-45">[38]</a></sup> whereas the slow cooling of the liquid Fe–FeS core causes convection and supports magnetic field generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hauk2006_75-9"><a href="#cite_note-Hauk2006-75">[67]</a></sup> The current <a href="/wiki/Heat_flux" title="Heat flux">heat flux</a> out of Ganymede is probably higher than that out of Callisto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-94">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Completed_missions">Completed missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Completed missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede_from_Pioneer_10_19.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="302" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ganymede_from_Pioneer_10_19.jpg/220px-Ganymede_from_Pioneer_10_19.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Ganymede_from_Pioneer_10_19.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganymede_from_Pioneer_10_19.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ganymede from <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i> (1973)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several probes flying by or orbiting Jupiter have explored Ganymede more closely, including four flybys in the 1970s, and multiple passes in the 1990s to 2000s.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i> approached in 1973 and <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i> in 1974,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pioneer_11_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pioneer_11-31">[25]</a></sup> and they returned information about the satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terraformers_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Terraformers-100">[91]</a></sup> This included more specific determination on physical characteristics and resolving features to 400 km (250 mi) on its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chap6_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-chap6-101">[92]</a></sup> Pioneer 10's closest approach was 446,250 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dmu.p10_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-dmu.p10-102">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> were next, passing by Ganymede in 1979. They refined its size, revealing it was larger than <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, which was previously thought to have been bigger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-103">[94]</a></sup> The grooved terrain was also seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_Mission_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager_Mission-104">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, the <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> spacecraft entered orbit around Jupiter and between 1996 and 2000 made six close flybys to explore Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Grand_Tour_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-The_Grand_Tour-37">[31]</a></sup> These flybys are G1, G2, G7, G8, G28 and G29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-12"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> During the closest flyby—G2—<i>Galileo</i> passed just 264 km from the surface of Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-13"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup> During a G1 flyby in 1996, the Ganymedian magnetic field was discovered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnetic_Field_Discovery_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magnetic_Field_Discovery-105">[96]</a></sup> while the discovery of the ocean was announced in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kivelson2002_28-14"><a href="#cite_note-Kivelson2002-28">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Grand_Tour_37-4"><a href="#cite_note-The_Grand_Tour-37">[31]</a></sup> <i>Galileo</i> transmitted a large number of spectral images and discovered several non-ice compounds on the surface of Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord1998_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-McCord1998-48">[41]</a></sup> The most recent close observations of Ganymede were made by <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">New Horizons</a></i>, which recorded topographic and compositional mapping data of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> and Ganymede during its flyby of Jupiter in 2007 en route to <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Horizons_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Horizons-106">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy2007_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy2007-107">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upcoming_mission_concepts">Upcoming mission concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Upcoming mission concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_Jupiter_System_Mission" title="Europa Jupiter System Mission">Europa Jupiter System Mission</a> (EJSM) had a proposed launch date in 2020, and was a joint <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">ESA</a> proposal for exploration of many of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s moons including Ganymede. In February 2009 it was announced that ESA and NASA had given this mission priority ahead of the <a href="/wiki/Titan_Saturn_System_Mission" title="Titan Saturn System Mission">Titan Saturn System Mission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.7897585_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.7897585-108">[99]</a></sup> EJSM consisted of the NASA-led <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Europa_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Europa Orbiter">Jupiter Europa Orbiter</a>, the ESA-led <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Ganymede_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Ganymede Orbiter">Jupiter Ganymede Orbiter</a>, and possibly a <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">JAXA</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Magnetospheric_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Magnetospheric Orbiter">Jupiter Magnetospheric Orbiter</a>. ESA's contribution faced funding competition from other ESA projects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa.41177_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-esa.41177-109">[100]</a></sup> but on 2 May 2012 the European part of the mission, renamed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer</a> (JUICE), obtained a L1 launch slot in 2022 with a <a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a> in the ESA's Cosmic Vision science programme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-juiceL1_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-juiceL1-110">[101]</a></sup> The spacecraft will orbit Ganymede and conduct multiple flyby investigations of Callisto and Europa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grasset_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-grasset-111">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voyager.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="626" data-file-width="800" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Voyager.jpg/200px-Voyager.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Voyager.jpg/300px-Voyager.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Voyager.jpg/400px-Voyager.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voyager.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i> spaceprobe</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Space_Research_Institute" title="Russian Space Research Institute">Russian Space Research Institute</a> is currently evaluating the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ganymede_Lander&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ganymede Lander (page does not exist)">Ganymede Lander</a> (GL) mission, with emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lander_workshop_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lander_workshop-112">[103]</a></sup> The Ganymede Lander would be a partner mission for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">JUpiter ICy moon Explorer (JUICE)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lander_workshop_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lander_workshop-112">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.20407902_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.20407902-113">[104]</a></sup> If selected, it would be launched in 2024, though this schedule might be revised and aligned with JUICE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lander_workshop_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lander_workshop-112">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Ganymede orbiter based on the <a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a> probe was proposed in 2010 for the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Science_Decadal_Survey" title="Planetary Science Decadal Survey">Planetary Science Decadal Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssb_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssb-114">[105]</a></sup> Possible instruments include Medium Resolution Camera, Flux Gate Magnetometer, Visible/NIR Imaging Spectrometer, Laser Altimeter, Low and High Energy Plasma Packages, Ion and Neutral Mass Spectrometer, UV Imaging Spectrometer, Radio and Plasma Wave sensor, Narrow Angle Camera, and a Sub-Surface Radar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssb_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-ssb-114">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another canceled proposal to orbit Ganymede was the <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moons_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Icy Moons Orbiter">Jupiter Icy Moons Orbiter</a>. It was designed to use <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> for power, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ion_engine" title="Ion engine">ion engine</a> propulsion, and would have studied Ganymede in greater detail than previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JIMO_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-JIMO-115">[106]</a></sup> However, the mission was canceled in 2005 because of budget cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-n.050207_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-n.050207-116">[107]</a></sup> Another old proposal was called The Grandeur of Ganymede.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pappalardo2001_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pappalardo2001-56">[48]</a></sup></p>
